WATCH FOR SIGNAL OF START OF PAI^DE AN^
rrrHE Call has arranged, with the approval of the executive committed in charge of ppepapationsfop the McKinley reception, to give signal from the I
J| dome of the Glaus Spreckels building the instant the column escorting President McKinley st^Pte streets this afternoon.
The Presidential train will arrive at 3:40 p. m. and the parade will move as soon as ; possible^ Watchers in the dome will be informed by direct wire
when command is given by the Grand Marshal, General Warfield, for the^ start to be made. The flag on the staff surmounting the dome will be unfurled,
bombs will be exploded and day rockets sent up. Owners of mills, factories, steamships, tugs, etc., are requested to arrange to have whistles blown,
and the general public is asked to make any contemplated demonstrations at that time,^ in hbnorof the coming of the President of the United States.
PROGRAMME TO BE FOLLOWED,
AS MRS. McKINLEY IS BETTER
MRS. McKINLEY'S health has considerably improved
since her arrival in this city. On Sunday night she slept
soundly and yesterday the reports of the doctors were
so cheering that the President left his wife for a few hours and
made a hurried trip to San Jose. During his absence Mrs.
McKinley slept uninterruptedly. The felon on Mrs. McKin
ley's hand is rapidly healing, and it is hoped that she will be in
a normal state of health in a few days. The President will
take part in all the events in San Francisco scheduled; com
mencing witri to-day.

Mrs. McKinley's Health.
Private Secretary Cortelyou received a
representative of The Call shortly after
the return from San Jose.
"Mrs. McKlnley, is much better," said
Secretary Cortelyou, "and she is improv
ing as rapidly as the doctors expect. "The
rest -is "doing her considerable good and
her hand is healing in good shape. Re
garding the programme for to-morrow,
th<? v - President will go to Palo Alto and
Burlingame in the morning and arrive in
San Francisco in the afternoon, unless
there is a change In Mrs/ McKinley's con
dition or a request is made by Mrs. Mc-
Kinley that the President remain with
her. The President; is much relieved by
the improvement in Mrs. â¢ McKinley's
health. He will not decide as to the trip
for to-morrow until the last^moment, but
will take the same steps as he did to-day.
The President is deeply touched by Â¦ the
sympathy of the people of San , Francisco,
and he hopes to ta^ce part in the original
programme of events commencing to-mor
row afternoon.*,'.

Cranks Turned Back by Guards.
From early morn till late at night a
crowd of sightseers loitered In Tront of
the temporary home of the nation's select
ed head. The police and detective guards
had a few cranks to deal with, some of
whom had 1 all kinds of quack nostrums to
offer to Mrs. McKinley. Needless to say
that the intruders werd not allowed to ap
proach the house.
A tiny little girl, with long blonde tress
es, asked to be allowed to pass the police
lines and her request was gratified. She
carried in her hand a bunch of exquisite
roses and said that she wanted to give
them to the President and Mrs. McKinley.
The little one was kindly received at the
house, and came away with sparkling
eyes and proud face.
At 1 p. m. President McKinley left the
Scott residence for his trip to San Jose.
He walked to his carriage with George*
E. Morse and was followed by Henry T.
Scott and Private Secretary Cortelyou.
The President checked the cheering of
the crowd in the vicinity of his carriage
by waving his hand.' With a clatter of
hoofs the mounted police escort formed
on each side of the President's carriage
and the start was made for the depot.
Chief of Police Sullivan and Captain
"Wittman drove ahead of the carriage, and
behind came carriages bearing Secret Ser
vice Agent Foster, the President's per
sonal bodyguard, lqcal detectives and po
lice officers.
President Cheered En Boute.
The sight of the closely guarded car
riage attracted attention all along the
route to the depot. Electric and cable
cars ' stopped, while their occupants
cheered the President Windows were
flung up and residents on the streets trav
ersed tendered welcome to San Francis
co's distinguished visitor.
The depot at Valencia street was
reached at 1:25. A force of police officers
kept the crowds at a respectful 'distance
from the platform, where the President's
train was waiting. A loud .burst of cheer
ing greeted President" McKinley as he
stepped from his : carriage and mounted
the steps of his car, "Olympia." Louder
and louder grew the cheers, and the Pres

By W. F\ Burke,
Staff Correspondent of The CalL
SAN JOSE, ilay 13.â Although It â¢
was for but a few minutes the
President's visit to San Jose waa
an event in the history of the .
city and one that will live long.
The President at 2:40; at
3:30 he was on his way back to the city:
but in the Intervening time there had
been a riotous, heartfelt, demonstration
which left the town in an uproar that
has not yet subsided., V-
In her reception dress the city i3
like some stolen arc of a rainbow.
It is not that the bunting flares in
the wind with hues unusually bril
liant and In quantity overstepping
previous display, nor is it In any
profusion or originality of decoration, but
It is in the combination of the carnival
red and gold mingling with the national
colors and the whole framed in a setting
of greensward, roofed with a sky as blue
as the sea and warmed in the light of. a
never failing sun. It is no trouble to dec
orate the city, for It needs but a palm
branch, flowers, a yard or so of bunting
and then all the summer dresses on tha
street. ' . .
Brilliant Night Spectacle.
It was at night, however, that the town
looked Its- best, for when the lights in tha
streets began to flash and the Chinese lan
terns to shine like glow worms among the
trees; when the air was filled with the
fragrance of flowers and soft music; when
the houses were alight at every window
and the streets were filled with a gay and
careless crowd, then the city caught the
carnival spirit and, filled with the excite
ment of the afternoon, began a festival
that promises to be the best of all.
The President came down from the city
after half-past 2, and as his car rolled in
from the north the remainder of the train
that contains the* other 1 members of his
party drew in from the south, and the en
gines met in the depot. The President and
the Cabinet officers alighted and the Sec
retaries at once paid their compliments to
the President and joined him on his way
to his carriage. The depot was crowded,
the streets outside were crowded and far
up the slant were more crowds on side
walk and roads, on steps and porches. In
windows and doors, all catching the cheer
that started at the station when the Presi
dent entered his carriage, it running up
the street ahead of him like a grass flra
before the wind. ' n
Tiny Flags Wave Welcome.
Up First street from the depot 2000
school children, each with a flag to wave,
were drawn up in line and they did wave
the little flags until the small arms ached
and they cheered and cheered until tha
small Voices cracked and the small
throats were dry. Then they Joined the
people, who broke into the street and fol
lowed the 'last carriage up to the grand
Btand.

PRESIDENT McKINLE Y will
make his official entry into San
Francisco this afternoon, ac
companied by the members of
his Cabinet. The metropolis of
the Golden State will tender a
Â¦welcome to the chief of 'the nation that
Â¦will live long in the memory of all -who
take part in it.
In the event of the President deciding
this morning that he -will not visit Palo
Alto and Burlingame to-day, he will this
afternoon proceed a few miles beyond
the city limits, in his private car, and
join the special train carrying the mem
bers of the Cabinet. From that time on
the official programme will be carried
out as announced.
At a late hour last evening Private Sec
retary Cortelyou stated that the Presi
dent would decide this morning as to
i visiting Palo Alto and Burlingame. The
President considers that San Francisco
Is the most important point in his trip
on the Pacific Coast and is most anxious
that nothing should delay the details of
the programme as arranged.
Regarding Mrs. McKlnley's condition
Secretary Cortelyou at midnight gave out
the following bulletin:
"Mrs. McKinley's physicians reported
to-night that their patient has passed a
more comfortable day than she has had
for several days past."
The marked improvement in the health
of Mrs. McKinley enabled the President
to make a flying trip to San Jose yester
day afternoon. In the space of four hours
and a half. President McKinley traveled
to San Jose, took part in the festivities
there and returned to the side of his wife.
Early yesterday morning it was report
ed at the Scott residence that Mrs. Mc-
Kinley had passed a comfortable night,
and the reassuring statements made by
Doctors Rixey and HIrschfelder on Sun
day night gave the President an opportu
nity to enjoy a well-earned repose. , ;
At 9 a. m. Mrs. McKinley awoke, and
her own words to the President were such
as to encourage the carrying out of the
programme of a visit to San Jose. The
President, however, decided to await the
coming of Dr. Hirschfelder and hear the
result of the consultation with Dr. Rixey.
Shortly before noon, the consulting phys
ician arrived at the house, and remained
.about a Quarter of an hour. As soon as
the doctors had informed the President of
the favorable condition of Mrs. McKinley
the President informed his private secre
tary that he would depart for San Jcse
at 1 p. m.
Word was sent to the railroad company,
and the President's special train was
placed in readiness at the Valencia street
depot

Scott residence for Mrs. MeKlnley yes
terday morning. The Park Commission
ers decorated the porch of the house with
splendid specimens of rhododendrons, and
cut flowers galore were received.
. Many ladles called at the residence and
left their'cards.^ but the only visitors ad
mitted were Mr. and Mrs* George E.

SAXTA CRUZ. May 13.â It was a
bitter disappointment to the Sea
side City to-day when the people
learned that the President would
not be with them. It was
said at flr*t that he would
be here; then this was contradicted
and then changed back again, until no
one knew just what to expect. It was
not until every one had alighted from
the President's train and It was
found that Mr. McKinley was not among
them that the people gave up hope.
There were carriages "galore" at the
station, and a band and the Santa Cruz
division of the Naval Militia.
The sailors kept the line at the sta
tion, escorted the party up Pacific avenue
to the courthouse, again stood guard
around the carriages, and, whea that duty
was done, boarded the special train that
took the party to and from the grove of
redwood giants, and did sentry duty
whenever necessary â and they did it well.
The division was under command of Lieu
tenant Gradie Doyle.
The school children had been gathered
from all parts of the county, and, looking
their prfjfiest. they lined up along the
street anr threw flowers at the members
of the party and waved flags in their
faces as they passed on.
Hay Speaks for His Chief.
Secretary Hay and Mrs. Hay and Major
J. P. Parker rode in the leading carriage.
The parade went along Pacific avenue to
Cooper, where the courthouse stands, and
there it halted while Mayor Parker made
a short address of welcome. At the con
clusion of Mayor Parker's words Secre
tary Hay arose In his carriage and replied
briefly. Said he:
Ladies and Gentlemenâ I appear before
you this morning In the most ungracious
attitude in which a human being can pre
sent himself to his friends â that is as the
We saw at Redlands the other day that
they sprinkled their streets with oil, and
we discovered the next day that they bored
for it at Los Angeles and got it. (Laugh
ter.) Really there is nothing that you have
not got here.,' We have met your people â '
your brave men and -women â we have met
the pioneers who builded and founded this
State, we have met all conditions of peo-r
pie, and wherever we have gone we have
seen smiling, happy and contented faces
and have heard the cheers of the school
Mr. Mayor; My Fellow Citizensâ "We have
had many warm and generous greetings as
we have Journeyed from the Potomac to
the Pacific slope, but none, have been more
Interesting, more generous and more mem
orable than the one which the people of
Santa Clara County and of San Jose accord
us to-day. (Great applause.) I observe that
I face not only this multitude of Ameri
cans, but . I face the headquarters of a
thousand old Ohioans, who. with my other
fellow citizens, give us welcome. We are
all proud of our States, and well we may
be, whether we come from the North or
whether we come from the South. "We are
proud of our birthplace and of our State
citizenship; but above all we rejoice in the
great nation, the glory, of Its achievements.
In the flag which represents liberty and
law, and the constitution of the nation that
shelters us all. (Applause.) |
We have seen everything In California;
we have eaten of your fruits- and your
fishes; we have tasted the perfumes of your
flowers; we have visited the ancient mis
sion churches where the altar of religion
was first raised and whose chimes have
sounded through the centuries their mes
sage of hope and benediction; we have
heard dashing waves of your ocean,
we have felt the sunshineâ and we have
been tanned somewhat by. its rays (laugh
ter), but we have all the time felt the
warm touch of your hearts.
Mr. McKinley's Happy Speech.
Mayor Martin delivered the address of
welcome. The cheers broke forth again
when the President stepped forward to re
ply. Said he:
Mr. McKinley for some time marveled
at the wealth of flowers, for there was a

ty-five feet up Into the trees and showing
all the colors that paint the gardens and
the hillsides. There were roses, sweet

The President was then escorted to the

cheer arose and shook the buildings, for
at last the people could look upon the
face of the nation's chief.
The grand stand was decorated in the

the carnival. It stood on the side of First
street and at the edge of St. James Park,
and from where he spoke the President
looked over to the courthouse, where the
steps were covered with people and .the

dotted with brilliant blossoms. And in
front of the Hall of Records, j adjoining,

OHIO'S GOVERNOR
RETURNS TO CITY
Executive and Staff Much
Pleased With Their Visit
to Santa Glara Valley
Governor Nash and staff arrived at the
Palace from San Jose last night shortly
after 11 o'clock. The party left the Garden
City at 9:30 p. m. and immediately after
arriving at the hotel went to their rooms
for a much needed rest.
The entire party is loud In its praise
of the beautiful scenery in and about San
Jose.
"The trip." said Private Secretary Sink,
"was an Ideal one. Governor Nash and
several of the party were taken for a
drive to the Big Trees in the Santa Cruz
Mountains in a tally-ho which was han
dled by an English gentleman, Mr. F. M.
Coope, In excellent style. We rounded
curves and went at breakneck speed
down steep grades in real 'days-of-old'
style. We enjoyed the trip immensely."
Governor Nash and staff will bo the
guests of Governor Gage and his staff this
morning. A trip around the bay has
been planned for the entertainment of the
visitinsr executive and his staff and the
ladies in the party.
The entire forenoon will be taken u;> by
the bay excursion, returning in time to
participate In the afternoon parade.
President McKiniey will be prompt at
the "launching of the Ohio." The view
from the grand stand of the San Francis
co Seat Company is unobstructed. " It ad-
Joins the Union Iron Works,- facing 400
feet on the bay. Be on time and get seat
ed early. Seats: on sale, at the office, 916

Co.'s. or at Newman's Richelieu, Geary

WILL BE FETED
AT MARE ISLAND
Plans for the Entertain
ment of the Visiting
Party of Congressmen
VALLEJO. May. 13.â The visiting Con
gressmen will leave the Clay-street wharf
at 9:30 o'clock Thursday morning on the
Government tug Golden Gate and will ar
rive at Mare Island at noon.' The tug is
to steam to the Heads and Fort Point. and
take in the Marin County shore, so that
as much of the bay as possible may be
seen. There will be sixty persons on
board, twenty-five of them being Con
gressmen. Senator Perkins and Congress
man Metcalf will be in the party.
Upon the arrival of the Congressmen
and their guests at the navy yard they
will be met by Rear Admiral Merrill Mil
ler, U. S. N., and staff, in uniform, and
the citizens' reception committee of Val
lejo. The Independence band will be in
attendance. The party wlU be escorted
to the handsome new gatehouse, where
luncheon will be served. This building is
now in the hands of the ladies' commit
tee and will be handsomely decorated
with flags and flowers. Not more than
an hour is to be spent there, after. which
the ladies of the party will be driven
about the yard, in carriages provided by
the committee and the Congressmen will
be escorted about the Government plant
and ships by the admiral and his staff and
the Vallejo committee.
Members of the Machinists' Union of
this city will present to Secretary of the
Navy Long a fourteen-karat gold card,
suitably inscribed, as a testimonial of the
many courtesies they, have received from
him.
Naval Constructor and Mrs. Zahm are
warm personal friends of President and
Mrs. McKiniey and have been invited to
spend F.ome time with them at the Scott
home. They wili go down to-morrow.

A light supper was served during the
evening and it was late when the music
ceased, the lanterns burned dim and flick
ered out, and those who had come to see
the President sought their homes.
Guests of the Evening.
The guests of honor were: Secretary of
State Hay, Mrs. Hay, Secretary of the
Navy Long, Mrs. Long, Postmaster Gen
eral Smith, Mrs. Smith, Secretary of the
Interior Hitchcock, Mrs. Hitchcock, Sec
retary of Agriculture Wilson, Miss Wil
son, President's Private Secretary Cortel
you. Mrs. Cortelyou, Assistant Secretary
Barnes, Henry T. Scott, Charles A.
Moore, Mrs. C. A. Moore, Miss May Bar
ber, Dr. R>*-ey, Mrs. Rixey.
The ball was in charge of the following
entertainment committee: Hon. O. A.
Hale, chairman: Hon. Alfred Holman.
Hon. George P. Snell, Hon. David Hender
son. Hon. J. D. Miner, Dr. H. C. Brown,
Mayor C. J. Martin and Director General
Greenmyer.
In the ballroom carnival colors were
entwined with the national colors on
every hand. The red and gold were
looped over the front of the music
stand, lighting the green of the waving
branches which gave distance and ro
mance to the music that came from
among them; afjnve, the flag lent its
touch of patriotism as well as or beauty,
and along the walls the gold and red and
the red, white and blue mingled in sig
nilicance of the dual sentiment of the
hour and the man of the occasion.
Around the chandeliers were twined smi
lax and red roses and sweet peas, hid
ing the metal and giving them the ap
pearance of hanging gardens. There were
more garlands and flowers and ferns and
palms down the hall and in the lobbies,
and the hotel never looked fairer than
when ready to meet the nation's chief.
The carnival ball to-night was perhaps
the biggest affair of its kind that San
Jose has ever given or ever seen. It was
intended the President should be there
and to prevent more than a comfortable
attendance the invitations were limited
and very hard to get. It was to have
been as much of a reception as a ball and
the most complete arrangements had
been made to avoid embarrassment to
hosts or honored guests, and to have the
entertainment which his presence would
give to the people there. In his place,
however, were the Cabinet officers attend
ed by their wives, and with Secretary
Hay as the principal figure, the affair
progressed as If the President had been
there.
Beauty of Decorations.
The greatest taste was shown in the
decorations that beautified the halls, the
lobby, the broad stairway and its pil
lars, the reception-room and the ball
room. In the reception-room rich red
roses clung to the curtains and hangings
over door and window, ropes of smilax
were festooned over walls and corners,
branches of pink hawthorn blossoms
broke the plainness of the arches, and
flowers of every hue and fragrance made
of the room a bower of beauty.
lanterns that shone like will o'
the wisps under the trees in the park
outside to-night. On the grass before
the hotel colored fires burned fiercely,
throwing their glare into the skies and
fhlning wierdly against the tree trunks.
And the road before the hotel was filled
Â¦with hurrying carriages, the canopied
Fieps of the perch and the porch itself
were crowded by peering, criticizing peo
ple, watching the guests as they entered,
while inside the halls rang with the light
laughter of people whose only care for
the moment was their own enjoyment.

PRESIDENT WILL %
REVIEW CADETS
TACOMA, May 13.â The Taklma Indians
will see President McKiniey and the mem
bers of his party on May 23. Word comes
from Fort Simcoe that at least 1800 gaily
attired men, women and children from the
reservation will visit North Yakima on
that day and do honor to the Great
Father. It is proposed that a war dance
be given for the amusement of the visi
tors. Plans have been made for a drive
through the â¢city, but many are asking
fcr the Indian dance. The Indians have
signified their desire to amuse the big
chief and his family with a war dance,
and this probably will be given.
The Yakimas have a grievance which
they wish to present. to the President. In
former surveys a large tract of land com
prising nearly 400,000 acres was taken
from their reservation, and thrown open
for settlement. Colonists located on the
lands and made homes. A large tract re
mains in timber and is one of the finest
bodies of . pine in the Northwest. When
a recent survey was made the lines were
properly located and the lands reverted
to the Indians. A commission sent out
from Washington, D. C, to make treaties
with the red men. offered them 25 cents
an acre for. the lands. As the lands are
very valuable the Indians desire to sell
their claims, and one of their number was
recently sent to Washington to see the
President. Â¦Â¦ Rev. Thomas Pearne, a native
Yakima, made the trip to the White
House and was greeted by Mr. McKiniey,
who. promised to send a new commission
to investigate the proposition.
The eonynissiqn has not arrived and the
Indians are getting uneasy about the mat
ter. They will call on the Great Father
as a tribe and show him their ponies and
fine feathers. They believe their lands
are as valuable as similar tracts would be
if owned by a white man, and have
spurned the offer of 25 cents an acre.

SAN RAFAEL, May 13.â The Marln
County McKiniey reception committee met
this evening and perfected arrangements
for the trip of the President to Sausalito
and Mount Tamalpaia on Thursday. The
principal point decided by the committee
was that absolute rest and seclusion will
be given the Presidential party on the

the line of the road up the mountain.

ACCOMPANYING the Presidential train- to arrive this afternoon are a number of representative "Washington corre
spondents, whose photographs are above presented. They may properly be termed the "historians" of this notable
Journey. ( *
, ' There are nearly 150 newspaper correspondents In "Washington City, representing all the important dailies of
this country, as well as some of the foreign newspapers. The President is extremely popular with the "Washing
ton press men and would have been glad if he could have extended an invitation to more of them, but the capacity of
the train was, of course, limited to a very few, so it was decided to bring only the representatives of the three routine pres3
associations furnishing news to all the dailies of the country, and the representatives of the three Washington papers. â¢
Howard N. Thompson of the Associated Press has for several years reported the proceedings of the House of Repre
sentatives for that association and he is well known to every member of Congress. . He is a graceful- writer and. some of
his biographical and obituary notices have attracted attention for their literary style. ' For several years he was Washing
ton correspondent for the Cleveland' Plain Dealer. ?
Henry LJtchfield West of the Washington Post is one of the best known and most accomplished newspaper men at
the national capital and one of the best informed. He is not only a most conscientious and energetic newsgatherer,
but is considered one of the most accurate and at the same time entertaining newspaper . writers - in Washington. He has
been president of the Gridiron Club, whose famous dinners are frequently attended by the President, Cabinet, Ministers,
Justices of the Supreme Court, Senators and the most talented and famous public men of the country. Mr.. West i3 uni
versally popular among his associates of the press. - . Â¦ Â¦ . Â¦
Walter E. Clark, one of the younger men, but one of the brightest, has not been so long In Washington, but Is-al
ready well known among public men. He represents the New York Sun's news-gathering bureau.
W. W. Price of the Washington Star is the representative of that paper at.tho White House. He has the confidence
of the official household and has contributed to his paper many exclusive stories. The columns of the Evening Star are
eagerly scanned by the corps of Washington correspondents every evening, and to the industry and ability 'of Mr. Price
they are indebted for many good White House "specials" which they have forwarded to their, respective papers.
R. H. Hazzard represents the Scripps-McRae Newsgatherlng Association. He is an entertaining writer and an es
pecially capable newspaper man in every sense of the word. He is comparatively a new man In Washington. Â¦ : Â¦ .
Another of the younger men is T. B. Lord of the Washington Times, whose work is rapidly making him well known to
the public men of Washington. He represents the Times in the press gallery.
Accompanying the party are also several photographers representing the various -weekly illustrated journals of New
York City. R. L. Dunn is connected with Frank Leslie's, J. C. Hemment with Harper's Weekly, J. H. Hare with Collier's
Weekly, and H. H. Strohmeyer with a photo-stereoptlcon company. These men stand high In their profession and their
names are, well known to readers of Eastern periodicals. . â¢
Morrell Marean is manager of the Western Union telegraph office in Washington and accompanies the party to see that
adequate wire facilities are enjoyed 'not only by the President and his official family, but also by the newspaper corre
spondents.
In the illustration above there are included three Stanford University, students and a Palo Alto committeeman who will
participate in the reception to the President at Stanford University.

JASPER- HARRELL DIES
IN HIS- VISALIA HOME
Leaves an Estate With an Estimated
Value of About Two Millitm
Dollars.
VISALIA, May 13.â Jasper Harrell died
to-night, aged 71 years. He left ail estate
valued at r about $2,000,000. ." He had two
children.
Mr. Harrell came to California In 1859.
He built the Vlsalla and Tulare railroad.
Two months ago he bought 35,000 acres in
Nevada, and his total realty at the time
of his death amounted to 100,000 acres. The
Nadeau Hotel in Los Angeles i3 among
Mr. Harrell's possessions. '
Mr. Harrell in 1837 married Miss Martha
Bacon. He was a plain, unassuming, prac
tical man.

Opium.
SEATTLE, May 13.â The medium of
transportation for the 585 pounds of opium
seized several days ago by the local cus
toms officers and pevhaps of tons of other
smuggled opium was discovered to-day by
Customs Inspectors Delaney and Brinker
and seized. It 13 a nameless naphtha
launch, twenty-five feet long. The craft
was found secreted on the tide flats in
a residence boathouse. The launch was
equipped with several gasoline tanks,- naif
a dozen cases of gasoline, numerous rub
ber sacks used as receptacles for the con
traband opium, rubber clothing, . dark
lanters, red lights and many paint, pots,
indicating that the launch has changed
color. There was a small arsenal of rifles,
ievolvers and shotguns.

PLANS TO CONSOLIDATE .'
TRANSATLANTIC LINES
Likelihood of the Atlantic Transport
and Leyland Companies
Being Amalgamated.- ;
NEW YORK, May 13.â President Ber
nard N. Baker of the Atlantic Transport
Line arrived here from London to-night
on the steamship Minneapolis. He. went
to England a short time ago for the pur
pose of securing, through the aid of J.
P.'* Morgan, the consolidation of theiAt
lantic Transport and Leyland lines, -the
fleets of which will aggregate 700,000 tons.
Should the deal be consummated the com
bined lines will be the largest in the
world.
President Baker admitted that the com
bine has as yet no legal standing, but
that there is a likelihood that in the near
future those controling the two lines may
find It to their interests to amalgamate.
ADMIRAL SCHLEY'S
VISIT IS SHORTENED
Hurries Home From London Because
of Serious Illness of His
Son*
LONDON, May 13.â Rear Admiral Schley
received a cable message to-day announc
ing, the serious illness of a member of his
family, and he. has, therefore, curtailed
his stay here and engaged passage on
board the North German Lloyd steamshlD
Kaiser Wilhelm der Grosse, which sails
from Southampton for New York May 15.
The admiral went to Soumampton this
afternoon. . . . â¢ Â¦ .
NEW YORK, May 13.â Rear Admiral
Schley's decision to take passage for New
York on the North German Lloyd steam
ship Kaiser Wilhelm der Grosse, sched
uled to . leave . Southampton on Wednes
day, three days in advance of the St.
Paul, on which he had planned to come
home, is due to the serious illness of Dr.
Winfield Scott Schley, son of the veteran
naval commander, i
Dr. Schley is undergoing treatment at
St. Luke's Hospital" for blood poisoning,
the result of a singular accident which
took place in his office- on Thursday last.
At one time his life was despaired of,' but
he " has gradually improved, and at the
hospital to-night it was said that he
would probably recover,', although he is
still very ill. . . . ,
Dr. Schley performed a surgical opera
tion in his office last Thursday. In hand
ling a scalpel the instrument slipped
through his fingers and, falling, point
downward, penetrated the leather of his
shoe and entered the flesh of the foot.
Two hours later unmistakable signs of
blood poisoning were apparent.

Special Dispatch to Tho Call.
LOS ANGELES, May 13.â A complaint
charging J. Ross Winters with murder,
on Saturday morning 1 , of his young wife
Grace .was sworn to to-day by r>etectlve
Walter H. Auble in City Justice Morgan's
court. Winters' arraignment â¢ probably
will take place to-morrow.
' The Coroner's jury which Investigated
the cause of Mrs. Winters' death to-day
returned an open verdict. The inquisition
at best was a perfunctory investigation
and from the testimony adduced the ver
dict could not well have been different
from what it was. It simply found that
the deceased met her death by a gunshot
wound, â¢
Dr. Jenkins, one of the surgeons who
performed the autopsy upon the body,
testifledr that Mrs. Winters could not pos
sibly have Inflicted upon herself the gap
ing wound which killed her instantaneous
ly. The testimony of E. W. Schmidt, gen
eral agent of the Fidelity Mutual Aid As
sociation of San Francisco, was rather fa
vorable to the prisoner, as it went to
show that the policy taken out In his
company by -Mrs. Winters recently was
merely to insure her in case of accident,
and not of death. As she had become de
linquent in her dues, she was even de
barred from the benefits allowed under
such a policy.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA ANA. May 13.â Perry Taylor of
Santa Ana has the unique distinction of
being one of seven men, so it la said,
who in the history of medical practice
has lived after undergoing an operation
for the removal of an entire lung.
Taylor is 26 yeurs old. About four years
ago he came to the conclusion that he had
consumption and consulted a local doc
tor, who treated him in the regular way
for a time for* tuberculosis of the lungs.
Later the physician became convinced
that Taylor's lung was affected with hy
dated cysts, and changed the treatment
accordingly.
An operation for the removal of the
cysts was then performed on Taylor, and
no less than 210 of them, ranging in size
from a small pea to that of a hen's egg,
were taken out through an aperture in
the patient's side. But more of them
grew, and although the operation ' of re
moving them was from time to time re
peated, it was at length decided to at
tempt a more heroic course of treatment
and remove the affected lung in its en
tirety.
The operation was successful so far as
removing the lung and the cysts was con
cerned, but a yawning void was left in
the man's anatomy.
Taylor Is getting used to doing with
only cne lung, and has devieed
a novel method of cleansing the pleural
cavity while the process of healing is go
ing on. His apparatus is a rubber tube
with a six or seven-inch long curved sil
ver nozzle, attached to an ordinary four
quart water bag. Just above Taylor's
waist on the right side is a vulcanized
rubber tube, which marks the center of a
large scar. Through this tube he Inserts
the silver nozzle and then turns on the
water, which contains a disinfectant.
After turning the nozzle around several
times so as to thoroughly irrigate his In
ternal economy it is withdrawn. Then
Taylor gathers himself up like a bucking
bronco, coughs, and there issues from his
side a column of water like 1 a miniature
geyser, which ceases at intervals only
when by a twist of Taylor's body the
stream is shut off from his side and finds
vent through his mouth with equal force
Taylor's general health is about normal
or as it was for a long time before he ever
dreamed of parting: with a lung, and he
feels confident that when his pleural
cavity is entirely healed he will be as
nearly all right as a one-lunger may ex
pect to be. Â¦ \j~
Unaffected by the Losa of
Half of His Breathing
Apparatus.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
REDWOOD CITY, May 13.-The trial of
the action in which Messrs. Jarboe,
Crooks. Hearfield and Bannister are plain
tiffs, Poniatowski and Bullock defend
ants, and William Grant intervenor, was
commenced in the Superior Court at this
place to-day. The litigation is over the
rent due from defendants for a marble
quarry in Tuolumne county for the year
1899. The defendants admit the indebted
ness, but do not know to whom to pay
the money, as a claim has been put In by
the Intervenor, Grant, who lias served
them with notice that he, as a member
of the Columbia Marble Company Is in
terested in $2500 of the amount '"lue, this
being one-half of the entire sum. The case
promises to be bitterly fought between
Grant and the plaintiffs.
When court opened this morning, Attor
ney Countryman moved to dismiss the
complaint of Grant, upon several grounds,
the principal one relied upon osing the
refusal of Grant to attend before a notary
public and have his deposition taken.
When served with the notary's subpena,
Grant declared that he had already made
arrangement to be away on the day set
for the examination, which was a Mon
day, but that he would go before the
notary on the Saturday preceding the day
fixed and would waive the legal notice.
This was not agreed to, and when Mon
day arrived Grant was in Stockton. Upon
the showing made, Judge Buck denied the
motion to dismiss.
David Hearfield, one of the plaintiffs,
was called to the stand and had not finish
ed his testimony when the court adjourn
ed. Considerable time was occupied, in
introducing documentary evidence in con
nection with the testimony of wit
ness.
Hearfield said that on September ID.
1898, he. Grant and Bannister leased the
marble quarry to Prince Poniatowski and
T. S. Bullock; that for the first year the
rent was to be $4000, which was paid at
the time the lease was made: that the
rent for the second year was to bo $b000.
which had not been paid. Evidence was
offered to show that in January, 18H9.
Grant sold his interest in the property to
Jarboe and Crooks for $50,000.
The lease referred to was' executed 1 , by
the Columbia Marble Company, through
the three members, Hearfield, Grant and
Bannister. Hearfield swore that it was
executed by these three, and that the
words, "Columbia Marble Company,"
were inserted after the execution of the
paper, and without his knowledge.
Grant claims an interest in the rent on
the ground that the lease was made by
the partnership, whose affairs were never
settled. This Hearfield denied, and swore
that on January 6, 1899, the partnership
business was settled, Grant at that time
paying $2523.06 as the amount aue from
him upon the settlement.
The case will go on to-morrow, and it is
expected that testimony of a highly sen
sational nature will be submitted.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN BERNARDINO, Cal., May 13.â The
San Bernardino Street Fair and Mer
chants' Carnival, in conjunction with the
Twenty-eighth District Agricultural Fair
Association, will open to-morrow evening
at 8 o'clock with the coronation of Queen
Marie, to be followed by an elaborate cor
onation ball , at the city park pavilion.
Booths of various descriptions are rapidly
being erected jj along Third, Court and
Dande streets. Thus far about twenty
booths have been built. Many of them
are of novel design and quite artistic.
The railroads have made low rates for
the occasion and every Incoming train
brings crowds. The fair will continue for
five days, during which time mirth will
reign supreme. ,
The streets. are being decorated In red,
yellow and white, the official fiesta colors.
As a result the city presents a gala ap
pearance. Hundreds of incandescent lights

SEARCHERS FIND THE HULK
OF ABANDONED SHIP COLUSA
Wreck Recently Reported by Indians on One
of the Queen Charlotte Islands .Proves
to Be That of the Long Missing Vessel
NANAIMO, B. Cm May 13.â News of the
discovery of the American ship Colusa.
Â¦which was abandoned more than a year
ago, was brought to Xanalmo to-nipht by
Captain Walbran of the Dominion Govern
ment steamer Quadra. Acting upon in
structions from the Department of Ma
rine and Fisheries, a party headed by
Mr. Dempster, manager of the Hudson
Bay tradins post at Skidegate: the Rev.
William S. Murray, a Presbyterian mis
sionary, and several others went out to
search the west coast of the islands for
The wreck recently reported by Indians.
Guided by the stories of a wandering band
of Hydahs they struck over toward Reu
nell Sound, the wildest and most desolate
part of the Island group. Only after a
careful search in the nooks along the
coast did they come upon the battered
hulk of the Colusa. It was completely
hidden from seaward by a tall cliff,
around the corner of which was a rocky
pocket sheltering the missing ship.
The discovery that the wreck is that of
the Colusa has but added to the mystery
of the derelict, for the Indians declare
that when it went ashore there were sea
men aboard, while the Colusa is known to
have been abandoned by her crew eight
een months ago.
The Colusa is piled high up forward on
the rocks and is split into three sections.
The mainyard hangs from the mainmast
in shreds of bleached canvas. The fore
mast and mizzenmast are gone close by
the board. The bottom is battered out and
ballast and cargo have slipped through.
Anchors, chain cables and windlass lie by
the starboard quarter in four feet of
water. Xot a vestige of stores, provisions,
furniture or gear is to be found aboard.
The furniture in the cabin is smashed to
atoms, except the principal table. The
crew appears to have stripped her thor
oughly before abandoning her.
Dempster and Murray searched all
around that side of Reunell Sound for
traces of any encampments, but found
nothing to indicate any human beings had
ever trod those sodden heaths. Dempster
says the ship looks as If she had been
there about a year, the timbers and cord
age being whitened and bleached. The
name of the vessel appears on the stem,
but the home port name is washed or
- Â¦
Special Dispatch to The CalL
broken, leaving only the letters "U. S.
A."
The reports of the two leaders of the
party being so circumstantial and the
other members having signed the reports
as correct, the Quadra did not proceed to
Reunell Sound, which is unsurveyed and
full of exceedingly strong currents. Cap
tain Walbran will make a full report of
the circumstances to the Department of
Marine.
The Indians who first reported the
wreck on the west coast said that two
bodies had been washed up and that there
were traces of a camp that had been in
habited by members of the crew.
CREW OF THE COLUSA SAVED
Ship Was Abandoned at Sea a Year
and a Half Ago.
The Colusa, whose dismantled hulk has
just been discovered, was well known in
San Francisco trade, having been owned
here for almost a generation. In 1SS9 she
was considered one of the most efficient
barks on the coast. At that time she was
under the command of Captain Backus,
who was one cf the principal losers when
she was abandoned in Alaskan waters. A.
H. Paul also was a heavy loser.
The Colusa sailed from this port on Sep
tember 6, 1S99, for the Hawaiian Islands.
During a storm she went ashore and was
got off with considerable difficulty. She
was taken to Kahului, where an exam
ination showed that her forefoot and part
of her shoe had been lost. It was decided
to send her to the Sound for repairs, and
accordingly, on September 21, she set sail
In ballast for Esquimau on what was to
prove her last voyage.
When the doomed bark neared Vancou
ver Island a storm arose and she was car
ried north before a hurricane. After float
ing about in a helples condition, at one
time in sight of Sitka's port, she was
finally abandoned by her captain and crew
on December 1L Four days later Captain
Evart, his wife and the entire crew of
thirteen men were picked up by the
steamer Sitka.
The bark Colura was built in Bath, Me.,
in 1873, was 1111 tons net burden, 197 fet 9
inches long, 3S fet 8 inches beam and 24
feet 2 inches deep.

CHANCE TO SMILE.
Some of these reports may be exaggerations. We
shall have to wait until complete statistics collected
by the census officials have been published to ' deter
mine how far the evil tendencies have gone. It ap
pears a foregone conclusion, however, that the census
will not be encouraging to British optimism.
' Another evil has been noted. It appears that as the
natives of the kingdom emigrate to find better lands
and employment aliens come in from abroad and take
tHeir places. A recent report ,says: "The country is
being filled up 'with impoverished peddlers, money
lenders, and hordes from Western Russia, Germany
and Austria."' It is added: "Mr. Balfour and Mr.
Chamberlain and others have at different times spoken
on the subject, but the movement goes on unchecked:
Whitechapel is no longer an English settlement, and
whole streets are now found in London and other
large cities where not an Englishman resides." *
- The emigration of men has seriously affected the
social conditions of the country by leaving within it
a large excess of women, who, having no opportunity
to marry, are crowding the avenues of labor. For the
purpose of remedying the evil a society has been
formed to promote the emigration of women to the
colonies, and the Government is expected" to assist
in the work, ".Â¦â¢''i *Â¦Â¦
; Another evil revealed by the census is the decrease
in the birth rate. It is reported that the returns show
that despite the increase of population as a whole,
the birth rate has so fallen off that there are now
500 less births daily than there were twenty years ago.
It is added that in England and Wales the loss in
what is called the "birth force" since 1874 has been
17 per cent, while in France, whose declining birth
rate has been the subject of so much speculation, the
loss during the same period has been only 14 per cent.
That' such a movement has taken place* was not un
expected, for it has" been' noted 'in every civilized
country.; Even in the United States, where there is
an abundance of cheap land and where almost all
kinds of rural industries are prospering, the growth, of
cities has of late been very much larger than the
growth of rural population. In no other country,
however, has the shifting been carried to such an ex
tent as in Great Britain, and nowhere else is there
any evidence of such a diminution of the farming
population as to seriously threaten the general wel
fare. Â¦ '
WHILE full returns from the British census
have not yet been made public, enough has
been given out to add a new source of
anxiety to the country. It is known the census will
show that while the large cities have increased in popu-.
lation, there has been a more than corresponding
decrease in the rural districts. Agriculture is no longer
a profitable occupation in the kingdom, and as a con
sequence the families who have tilled the farms are
either moving to the cities or are emigrating to the
colonies or to the United States.
THE BRITISH CENSUS.
JOIN IN THE WELCOME.
AS was announced yesterday by Grand Marshal
Warn" eld, it is arranged that signal be given
by The Call from the dome of the Claus
Spreckels building the instant the column escorting
the President starts from Third and Townsend streets.
Bombs will be exploded, day rockets sent up and the
flag unfurled from the flagstaff.
At that moment the President of the United States
becomes the guest of San Francisco. It is desirable
that the whole city join in the welcome. Therefore
the owners of mills, factories and steamships should
immediately take up the signal and repeat it by
whistles and flags, so that the whole peninsula may be
made to reverberate the glad welcome. Every
â¢whistle and every flag should be made to unite in sig
naling the moment of the reception.
Let everybody be prepared. Let the people give
voice to their delight to honor the chief magistrate
of the republic. Let every whistle sound forth the
summons to patriotic throngs to wait along the streets
to greet the President with acclamations. Let not
one be silent. The universal joy should find expres
sion by the universal voice.
A NEW POSTAL RULING.
CONSIDERABLE attention has been given in
the East to a new ruling of the Postoffice
Department concerning writing contained in
any package or parcel intended for fourth class mails.
As samples of merchandise are included in that class
of mail, the ruling affects the business of a large num
ber of merchants and manufacturers, it having been
the custom for tlje past ten or twelve years for the
shipper to mark the samples sent through the mails
with the price and a few words of description. In
1894 a notice was sent out from the Postoffice Depart
ment to the effect that written or printed marks indi
cating the price were admissible on samples of mer
chandise sent as fourth class matter, but the' new
ruling puts an end to the practice.
A report from Washington says: "The assistant at
torney general for the Postoffice Department has ad
vised the postal authorities of his construction upon
the old law of 1888, and it is to the effect that such
marks must not be made upon the matter itself, or
upon anything inside the wrapper. He says that the
sender may write upon the wrapper or cover of fourth
class matter, or upon a tag or label accompanying it,
the marks of description and the price. His interpre
tation has been adopted by the department as law,
and any fourth class. matter prepared for mailing in
conflict with this ruling will be subject to first class
postage. Though it is nowhere specified, the word
'from' and possibly other words which may be neces
sary to indicate precisely the origin and nature of the
merchandise will be admissible when they are in the
proper place."
Commenting upon the new rule the Boston Tran
script says: "A fine distinction is made, it appears,
in the postal laws between samples of merchandise
and fourth class mail matter, but in the application of
the law it is difficult to observe this distinction, as
samples are included in the list of fourth class matter.
At the Boston postoffice the letter from the Third
Assistant Postmaster General in 1894? authorizing
the price to be written or printed upon the sample
itself will be looked upon as no longer in force, and
all marks must be written on the outside 'of the wrap
per or upon the accompanying tag."
The cause for the change in the ruling- is not ap
parent, but it is probable the privilege of putting
printed or written words on samples of merchandise
has been abused by .unscrupulous parties, Â«and the
Government has found it necessary to deny the privi
lege hereafter. The change is not . of any great im
portance, but all concerned .will do well to take note
of it in order to avoid having to pay .higher postage
than is necessary in sending samples properly, pre
pared for mailing. '
THIS WAS A HERO.
ALL the heroes are not in uniform, their action
quickened by war drums and influenced by all
mankind as an audience, and history as a re
corder.
The negro in Indianapolis who was caught by steam
carelessly let into a boiler where he and a companion
were working was of real heroic fiber. As the de
stroying scald poured upon them they ran for the lad
der to the manhole in their chamber of torture and
death. Phelps reached it first, but drew back and
cried to his mate, "You first, Jim; you are married,"
and Jim went first and was saved, while the deadly
steam cooked Phelps' flesh as he followed, and he
was drawn out of the hole to die. With his la^t
breath he said, quietly, "It was Jim's right to go first;
he is married." The man who in such peril, could re
member the wife of his mate and her mourning, and
die willingly rather than bring widowhood and sor
row into a humble home, was a greater hero than all
the commanders that have led armies. Greater love
than this hath no man, to die for his. friend.
There was no glory in sighf, no renown; none of
that reward in history which enter into the motives of
some. There was nothing but awful death that an
other might live. Â¦
Let "all men remember this lowly laborer/ V His
place is in the great company of martyrs who have pa
tiently tasted death that men might live or principles
be promoted. He was of that race we call inferior,
but his black skin covered a heroic soul.
In the center of the city of his sacrifice rises a
stately monument to the soldier dead of Indiana. " It
perpetuates the memory of George Rogers Clark and
the high action at old Vincennes, and reminds the
beholder of the defenders of the flag in many wars.
It is right that it is there. But let us trust Indianapolis
to rear another less lofty shaft over the dust of this
dusky hero, and carve upon its base: "It was right
for Jim to go first; he is married."

DIVORCES AND MARIIIAGES-C. W.
, F., Oakland, Cal. There are no lato com
pilations that will give "the percentage
of divorces to marriages In California and
in every State In the union at this time."
To obtain such statistics would take a
great deal of time and Involve much la
bor. Take California, for Instance; It
would be necessary to communicate with
each of the nfty-four County Clerks and
Recorders to ascertain how many mar
riage licenses were recorded and how
many divorces were granted, and the
same process would have to ba followed
in every State and Territory. Then to
obtain "the rate of increase In divorces
per year" would necessitate a search of
the records for a number of years.
PATENT LEATHERâ R. E. T., City.
The first coats for the Japan for patent
leather are made with Unseed oil and
Prussian blue, boiled together for some
hours; the last coat or varnish, with Un
seed oil similarly boiled. Each coat Is
separately dried at a temperature of from
170 |to 180 deg. Fahrenheit and rubbed in
the leather by hand, the skin being nailed
to the surface of a board. The process
Is a very delicate one, requiring special
knowledge in each part of the operation.
To preserve the gloss and prevent crack
ing of such leather, melt wax with a lit
tle oil of turpentine, Olive oil and lard,
mix thoroughly, and when cold It should
be a thick paste. Vaseline Is excellent.
Allow it to remain on for one-half hour,
then rub dry with a piece of canton flan
nel.

CAUTOBNIAXS DT NEW YOBK.
freight, and our producers must get their exchange. Time, tide and trade 'wait not even for
the President, who, most of all who have held the great office, has given his attention to
the economic conditions which generate trade and has done so much to make us too busy
to tarry long, even in saluting him.
He gets here the sincerest flattery we can offer, in the energy and enterprise, the time
and talents given up to the operation of the prosperous conditions he has promoted. His
praises are not merely sounded by the trumpeters, but are uttered in the packing sheds
and the rattle of trade on the crowded docks. They are written in the passbooks of the
savings banks, where labor has accumulated millions since he became President, and in the
diapason of steam whistles on land and water, which summon men to labor, release them
to refreshment and utter "au revoir" as the Pacific liners steam through the Golden Gate
to the Orient.
r s O-JDAY San Francisco salutes the President and Cabinet, and srives the freedom of
the city to the Government of the United States. In. a certain very, definite sense
this city will become, for severaldays, the capital of the United States, for the
President and his constitutional advisers will here consider and dispose of such Fed
eral business as presses for attention. During that time the executive mansion is planted
on* one of our sightliest hills, overlooking bay and ocean and the enclosing 1 mountains.
The tour of the President has been wisely planned. After traversing the picturesque
American Sahara, where the misty distance and-inviting mirage pictured the actual scenes
that were ahead, he entered the American Riviera in Southern. California, where the re
sources of nature are ample for the production of all that exhibits the floral charms of cli
mate, and where the sunshine tempts to outdoor adornment and the enjoyment of life's
leisure. It is a scene that cannot be duplicated on the planet.
Emerging from it, to-day the President enters San Francisco, the busy, noisy, push
ing, progressive, industrial, commercial and financial center of the State's productive. zone,
and the New York of the Pacific Coast, destined to remain always , the metropolis of this
side of the continent, and the greatest city and center of Western civilization on the Pa-
Its development is due, primarily, to the productive part of the State, which is wholly
tributary to it. That production at first was gold. The placers were, generous yielders,
and the dust came here to pay for everything, from pleasure to provisions. Then the ledges
were unlocked and their treasure flowed to San Francisco. Here came the tens of millions
dug from .the depths of the Comstock, and the deep mines of the mother lode send their
monthly yield to us. As the nobler uses of the soil were discovered, and field, orchard and
vineyard took the place of the. flocks and herds that had grazed our valleys, the real gold
mine of the State was discovered. The productive soil of Northern California is a ledge
that never pinches, a placer that is never washed out, a source of wealth permanently po
tential, that will be in action after the gold-bearing rock has been followed so far into the
earth that the miner can go no deeper and live. . Then the grapes will still purple on the
vine, and the fruit will blush in the orchard, and the grain will billow like a golden ocean in
the breeze. The extension of irrigation will render the producer more and more indepen
dent of one season's drought, and the wide distribution of electric power, generated by our
mountain streams, will harness a new force to the processes that will continue to take
wealth from this exhaustless store. :
These causes make us a busy j preoccupied, commercial people. We are working, and
may lend ourselves shyly to holiday uses. But what we lack in grace and form we make
up in heartiness and sincerity. Our position, the great things that are with us daily as the
factors of a vast production, our study of markets, of competitors, of consumers and cus
tomers, .make us a very practical people, and our garlands may not be on straight; but we
can pay for them. As-becomes the metropolis of the greatest ocean and of the edges of
two continents, our population is cosmopolitan. The President can here see every order
of mankind, every kindred and tribe and tongue, from a Parsee to a State of Maine Yankee.
All are here from the torrid, temperate and frozen zones and the islands of the sea. Men are
here who were born under every flag that flies, and some who were born under no flag ex
cept the milky baldric of the skies and the stars that twinkle.. Yet all are living in obedi
ence to our laws and in loyalty to our standard.
If the President find the sidewalks more given up to packages of merchandise than
to decoration, it is because the great steamers that come and go between our docks and
the ports of Asia, Australasia, the three Americas and the far islands must get. their

â¢ ' 7 Tendency of British Rule.
It will bo noted that these many and
very varied forms of government are all
precisÂ© degrees in the graduated scale of
political development and national evolu
tion. The aim of the mother country is
to make of her children nations self-gov
erned and able to take care of themselves.
The greatest mistake Great Britain ever
made was her famous misrule of her erst
while great American colonies. Her sig
nal defeat and discomfiture taught her the
true method of dealing with her colonies.

How Great Britain Watches Over the Vast

AUTHOR OF "THE ARMS AND BAD GES OF THE BRITISH COLONIES."

Xm,- GOVERNMENT OF BRITISH COLONIESâ A PROFIT AND LOSS
ACCOUNT.
.The British empire comprises one-fifth
of the known land surface of the globe.
The number of people who live under her
flag is represented by the enormous total,
in round figures, of 400,000,000.
The possessions of Great Britain acros3
the seas are of three kindsâ one, colonies;
two, dependencies; three, protectorates.
The colonies .are of three classesâ one,
crown colonies; two, colonies with repre
sentative Institutions; three, self-govern
ing colonies.
Kinds of British Colonies.
1. Crown colonies â The crown has entire
control . of legislation and appoints the
Governor. The administration is carried
on by public officers under the direction
of the principal Secretary of State for the
Colonies.
Such are, in Asia, Straits Settlements,
Hongkong and British New Guinea; In
America, British Guiana, British- Hondu-i
ras, Falkland Islands, Fiji, the Pacific isl-/
ands and Trinidad and Tobago; in Africa,
the Seychelles Islands, Sierra Leone, La
gos, Gambia, Gold Coast and St. Helena.
2. Colonies with representative institu
tionsâThe crown appoints the Governor
and has a veto on legislation. They have
representative institutions suited to their
several characteristics and circumstances;
but the Secretary of State for the Colo
nies has control over the public officers.
Such are, in Asia, Ceylon and Borneo;
In America, the West Indian Islands of
Bahamas, Barbados, Bermuda, Jamaica,
Leeward and Windward Islands; in Afri
ca, Mauritius; '.n Europe, Malta. (The last
two are also quasi-crown colonies.)
3. Self-governing coloniesâ The crown
appoints the Governor and has a veto on
legislation. They possess representative
Institutions and responsible government.
The Secretary of State has no control
over the administration nor the public of
ficers. !
Such are the dominion of Canada and
its twelve component provinces, the com
monwealth of Australia and its six com
ponent provinces, Newfoundland, New
Zealand, Cape of Good Hope and Natal.
. Dependencies.
â¢ These are of two kindsâ first, the empire
of India; second, "sphere of influence."
1. The empire of India is in no sense a
colony; it Is a splendid possession of the
British crown and an integral part of the
British empire. It consisis of thirteen
provinces and a number of scattered naval
and military stations. These are Aden,
with the Arabian and Somali coasts pro
tectorates, and Wei-Hai-Wei in China.
The native states' o' India are more or Ies3
dependent upon the imperial Indian Gov
ernment. These number C50.
2. "Spheres of influence," so called, are
Afghanistan, Beluchistan. Bhutan and
Nepal in Asia, and Egypt with the British
Soudan in Africa.
Protectorates.
These are all situated in Africa and are
a3 follows: Ashanti, Barotseland, Basu
tbland, British East Africa, British South.
Africa. British Central Africa. Bechuana
land, Nigeria (North and South), Khode
sia (North and South), Somaliland, Ugan
da, Witu and Zanzibar. - Each of these
forms a separate administration under
British officersâ some military, some com
mercialâbut subject to the neighboring
colony and under the control of the colo
nial or foreign officersâ tho latter when
questions relating to the frontiers of the
colonies and dependencies or protectorates
of foreign staiSB are concerned.
Other British Possessions.
The status of the Transvaal and Orange
Kiver Colony is at the present moment
unsettled. They will ultimately be gov
erned as are the Cape of Good Hope and
Natal.
Gibraltar Is a fortress rather, than a
colony. Ascension is ordered as a battle
ship of the British navy. Cyprus, though
nominally a port of the Ottoman empire,
and paying an annual tribute to the Sul
tan, is administered by British officers,
and, for all intents and purposes, it is
treated as a British colony. There are
also very many otWer islands and stations
which belong to the British empire and
are scattered everywhere. These are
places of call for merchant vessels, coal
ing stations, telegraph stations, observa
tion stations and tho likeâ useful in times
of peaco and tnvaluablo in time of war.
Such are Tristan da Gunha and Walfish
Bay. These arc administered, or visited
regularly, by British officials. .
Hongkong, Â£47,000; Mauritius. Â£21,500.
There are also smaller contributions from
a few other colonies.
Financial Position of the Colonies.
In far the greater number of the Brit
ish colonies the revenue is in excess of
the expenditure. The Dominion of Can
ada heads the list by a volume of above
Â£5,000,000. In- Western Australia the reve
nue and expenditure balance each other.
In the other Australian provinces the rev
enue is in excess of the expenditure. At
the Cape of Good Hope and in Natal,
owing 1 to the Boer war, there are no exact
figures to hand. Among colonies where
the expenditure Is in excess of the reve
nue are the Gold Coast, Jamaica and
Newfoundland. In India -there was in
1S98-99 an excess of revenue over expendi
ture of about Â£2,000,000. All the colonies
and dependencies are in the enjoyment of
public debts, small or great, and all have
had to resort to the mother, country for
loans, also great or small. These, how
ever, in nearly every instance are under
going a course of reduction.
The Gold Coast Colony.
The Gold Coast. Colony comprises the
coast of the Gulf of Guinea to the west
of the Niger. It has been in the undis
turbed possession of ; Great .Britain for
three centuries. Although the best gov
erned, and, until recently, the most pros
perous of the "West African colonies,, it
has been going through times of trouble
which have retarded its development. To
gether with the northern territories and
the two Nigerias, it sustained the last
'Ashanti war. This native state is at
length subdued, but the civil service esti
mate bears witness to the depression
caused by that enterprise. \ - <&
In a few years, doubtless, the Gold
Coast Colony will cease to be a weak link
financially in the mighty chain of British
Condition of Jamaica.
The history of Jamaica is checkered and
for the most part sad. It has been a Brit
ish possession since 1670. The population
numbers nearly 1,000,000, the majority be
ing black or colored. The period of 1856
1865 was one of anarchy. The blacks rose
against their masters, who -were hope
lessly beaten. The land, which rapidly
went out of cultivation, changed hands,
and the colored races ruled the Island.
During the period of 1S65-1888 a fresh start
was made. A new fiscal system came
into operation; the adjustment of taxa
tion, amelioration of the law of property,
in education and in internal communica
tions led to revived prosperity. But the
present day finds the blight not yet quite
removed. The revenue is greately affected
by the low price of sugar. In 1881 the
price was 29 shillings a hundred weight,
and" now It is only 10 shillings. Beet-root
sugar, produced in Europe under govern
mental aid-, floods the world's markets
and against this bounty-fed antagonist
cane sugar has no chance.
The year 1898 was marked by growing
prosperity. There was a considerable rise
in the price of cane sugar through the
imposition by the United States of coun
tervailing duties upon bounty-fed sugar.
Jamaica is splendidly placed for the ex
pansion of trade in spite of hurricanes and
other destructive visitants. The soil is
fertile and the climate salubrious. Social
condition and civic efficiency are alike ex
cellent. The people are sturdy workers
and are becoming . well educated. The
time cannot be far distant when Jamaica
will take her place among the prosperous
colonies of the British empire.
The Newfoundland Fisheries.
Newfoundland is quite the oldest child
of Great Britain's vast colonial family.
Discovered in 1497 by John Cabot, it has
ever since belonged to the British crown
and empire. In 1500 French, fishermen
made a settlement on the northwest shore.
By the peace of Utrecht in 1713 the sov
ereignty of Great Britain was recognized,
but the French were allowed to retain
the right to land and to dry fish on the
north and west shores. This right has
very greatly retarded the development of
the island. The expenditure of the colony
exceeds the revenue by Â£150,000 a year
and the public debt is considerable. Pros
perity can only be secured by the Impe
rial Government coming to terms with
France. / *

NEW YORK, May 13.â Under reassuring
news from London to the effect that a
basis of settlement with Northern Pacific
shorts had been agreed upon and that J.
P. Morgan was lending the stock "flat"
for delivery in that market, the Stock Ex
change began the day strong.
The first trading was well distributed
throughout the list at prices generally
higher than on the close Friday. Union
Pacific sold at 116 a few minutes after 10
o'clock, showing a gain of $40 a share as
compared with Thursday's low point.
Developments In Northern Pacific are
awaited with the keenest interest, but It
was the general opinion In Wall street to
day that It would be some time before tha
question of control would be definitely set
tled. It Is believed that one party to tha
controversy holds control through the pre
ferred stock and that several Intricate le
gal points are Involved concerning the
right of the directors of the company to
retire the preferred stock at par and thus
deprive it of its voting power.
The rise In Union Pacific was accompa
nied by a similar but not so extensive
movement in Amalgamated Copper, which,
opening at 116%, sold up to 120%, an ad
vance of 5*i as compared with Friday's
clone. It subsequently declined to US 1 *.
There were selling orders In Union Pa
cific above 120, and the stock fell back to
H6V2. but an immediate rally followed. To
ward noon the market became quieter and
weaker and generally declined on small
transactions. There was no sale of North
ern Pacific common until 11:30, when a
sale of 10O shares was made at 160, an ad
vance of ten points from Friday's last
Â¦ale.
LONDON, May 13.â The Stock Exchange
committee and J. P. Morgan have come
to the rescue of the situation, and the dif
ficulty in which operators and brokers
who have given calls for Northern Pacific
for the end of May and July found them
selves is satisfactorily settled.
The committee decided that the "buying
In" of Northern Pacific common and pre
ferred be suspended for the present. This
means that the stock need not be deliv
ered Immediately. The committee also
fixed the maklng-up price of Northern
Pacific common at 140. Morgan la lending
Northern Pacific at "evens" â that is, for
nothing.
Decided satisfaction has been expressed
on the Stock Exchange here over the ten
tative settlement of tne Northern Pacific
crisis. /The representatives here of Kuhn.
Locb & Co., Powell, Jackson & Georger,
paid to a representative of the Associated
Press:
There Ia much nervousness In view of thÂ«
fact that the relief may be only temporary and
that another crisis may be precipitated by the
action of either Morgan or Harrlman. The sus
pension of the settlement' announced by> the
committee this morntr.s Is of indefinite dura
tion, but each day gained nelpa the liquidation
of accounts. The brokers who are th<j worst
affected generally hold options on Northern Pa
cific and they can realize In June and July. In
the meantime salvation depends on the leniency
of Mr. Morsran and Kuhn. Loeb & Co.
PROTECTING [WESTERN ROAUS
"Worked Out.
Flan Relating to Burlington Is Being
NEW YORK. May 13.-The Evening
Post says: It Is believed that some ar
rangement will be worked out by which
Burlington will be held so that It cannot
possibly be operated to Injure any of tho
western railroads. That is, the Union
Pacific will not only get an interest In the
property, but the St. Paul, which next to
the Union Pacific was most cerlously
jeopardized by the passing of Burlington's
control to the Northern transcontinental
lines, will share in the Burlincton con
tract, so that it will be removed absolute
ly as a disturbing- factor In the railroad
situation. It Is not believed that J. P.
Morgan & Co. will be displaced as the
Interest responsible before the public for
the management of the property, but
there was some suggestion to-day that
the alliance between Hill and Morgan,
which ha3 been of only about six months*
duration, will be ended. There is good
grounds for saying that while Hill has
probably retained the large interest In
Northern Pacific which he acauired last
summer, when the stock declined below 50
on the reduction of the dividend, the most
influential of Hill's friends in the direc
torate have liquidated their holdings.
They were, with Hill, who ia not a North
ern Pacific director, the most enthusias
tic advocates of the Burlington deal. This
was long opposed by the older Northern
Pacific people.

Champions to Play Checkers.
A checker contest for the championship
of the Pacific Coast will be played at the
Mechanics' Institute next Sunday from 9
to 12 in the morning and 1 to 5 in the af
ternoon between H. M. Angell and W. J.
Bowe. The contestants are two of the
best players on the coast. The match is
to settle a controversy of five years'
{standing.

Alcazar.
Milton Royle's Interesting comedy drama
"Friends" was again presented at the Al
cazar Theater last night to a crowded
house. It was produced â¢ the previous
Monday night on the occasion of the bene
fit of -St. Brigld's Church and the cast
last night was the same. The play Itself
is so admirably constructed, blending high
class comedy with tragedy so effectively
that the feelings of the audience are kept
alternating from grave to gay throughout
the four acts. Howard Scott as Hans
Otto, the drunken father, again scored a
great success and was honored with a
special call before the curtain after the
close of the third act with Its intensely
dramatic situation. It is an exceedingly
clever piece of character work. Joseph
Kilgour in the comedy part of John.
I-eden Jr. and Barton Hill as John Paden
Sr. were admirable and their dialogue was
refreshing. Charles Bryant as Adrian
Karje and Richard Scott as Harold HXnt
iiiff were well suited to the parts. Miss
Lila Convere was Marguerite Otto, and
her art was forcibly demonstrated in the
lights and shades of the part which she
&o elegantly portrayed. Miss Agnes Ran
k^n made a charming "little mother" and
Miss Georgie Cooper and Miss Gufba Dau
det. although they had little to do, helped
to make the piece successful.

Grand Opera-House.
If ever there existed a tolerably good
play, hampered with an intolerably bad
title, that play is "Government Accept
ance." There Is nothing in the name to
suggest the humor that bubbles from the
lips of that realistic liar, N.' Tecumseh
Sheridan, or that purrounds with a halo of
merriment the wabbling figure of Cap
tain Paul Rainerd. These are the funny
characters that atone for the bad name
of the play and they are well handled by
Eromett Shackelford as the soldier and T.
J. McGrane as the jovial sea captain. The
serious roles are those of Robert Gordon,
the electrlciaH, who has a boat to sell
to the Government, and Richard Bund
age, his rival, and the execrated villain of
the comedy drama. Gordon, in the hands
of Jack Webster; is a hero -in plain life,
who carries with him from start to finish
the sympathy of the audience and Brund
age, as portrayed by Asa Lee Willard,
won not a few hisses for his clever imper
sonation. Florence Stone has the oart of
a vain but good hearted woman and Ma
thilde Choate does the widow Kingsley
most sympathetically.

fis-jy^HE heat was terrific at the Cen-
I tral Theater, last night. Belasco
Â§ & Thall's stock company, aug
11 mented by Terry McGovern, Danny
Dougherty and Joe Humphreys, in
the rare, perfervid, lurid melodrama, "The
Bowery After Dark," produced the excess
of thermal extrayagance. Four thermom
eters on' tho other side of the street
coughed their last and all the atmospheric
registers in the neighborhood showed the
ghost in a futile endeavor to keep pace
with the fearful, torrid drama that ran
four acts within the inclosure.
McGovern is well worth the seeing as a
fighter and doubly so as an actor. Inside
the "roped arena" in a single night but
one man falls to his vicious, earth-lower
ing swings and jolts. On the stage the
pugilistic vandal gives free morpheal
transportation to armies of villainous bel
ligerents. Big men with heavy, basso
rrofundo voices, Chinese conspirators and
all the rabble of the real, palpitating melo
drama fall before his mighty, forceful left
and right. There never was such a drama
as the post-sunlighted Bowery. It teems
with action, incident and situation and
must be seen to be appreciated. The 121
pound "Terrible" is in snd of it.
First of all the scenic effects is a saloon
on the bowery in which the plot is
hatched. The bad man. and he is the
perfection of black-striped villainy, wants
to sell his wife into slavery. On the pro
gramme he goes as a "refined villain."
Any old thing goes with himâ murder,
pills or strangulation. His wife is a
piece of monumental patience waiting for
his demise, naturally or violently secured.
It comes in the last act, when the Chin
ese accomplice perforates a dorsal artery
and then the entire company grows gay
over the prospective funeral, and the
rougti rider, Joseph Howe, holds Flora
Morris, the bad man's wife, in his arms,
whispering: "At last, at last, I have
you." All this happens at the ringside
after the "Terrible Terry" puts the re
doubtable lightweight champion, "Frankie
Kerne," out of business. Then comes the
denouement. Terry takes his wife unto
himself. The good man kisses the good
woman and the spectatorsâ composed of
steam-beer drinkers from the south side â
shout "long live the champion!"
There is not a poor one in the part.
They all shine with a light that possesses
sucli heat penetrating powers that much
fog and iced drinks are necessary to cool
the head after taking.
Opium joints, street scenes and the
ringside with sports and others congre
gated outside the ropes make up the
spectacular element of the play. Terry,
the boy wonder, evolutes from a Bowery
cry shoulder-swinger, to a Tuxedo-clad

"WASHINGTON, May 13.â Conferences
were in progress In this city to-day be
tween President O'Connell of the Interna
tional Association of Machinists, Presi
dent Gompers of the American Federation
of, Labor and officials of several smaller
working trades other than the machinists,
considering the moral and financial sup
port to be given the proposed strike of
machinists.
' President O'Connell said the outlook was
very encourag'ng for a successful strike
in many localities. A number of firms
not now complying with the arrangement
entered into between the machinists and
the association of employers as to a nine
hour day signed to-day, and O'Connell
predicts that a large number of these will
have signed by Hay 20. He says the
Btrlko will affect at least 100,000 men di
rectly and moiv than that number of al
lied mechanics indirectly.
It is stated that the machinists will be
supported in lliclr struggle by the Ameri
can Federation of Labor.
NEW YUKK. May 13.â Secretary Wilson
of district 13 or the National Association
of Machinists was interviewed to-day con
cerning tlio order sent out Saturday by
National PreaMmt .Tamon O'Connell fit
Washington railing a strike of all ma
chinists throughout the country on Mon
day next unless Â»;mployers algned the
agreement for a working day of nine
hours and an Increase of 12V2 per cent in
wnges.
"There is HttU- doubt in my mind," said
Wilson, "that the strike will actually take
place, but the employers have until Satur
day next to come to our terms. That is the
last working dixy before the date set for
the order to go into effect. The figures
given in the Washington dispatches are
wrong. They fay the strike, if it takes
place,. will affect only 150,000 men. On the
contrary, it will affect close to G0O.OO0 men.
"The machinists alone who will be
called out will bo 150,000, but all trades de
pending on the machinists for their tools
will be brought to a standstill. An erro
neous opinion ptevails that this strike will
mean the practical stoppage of all work
on public buildings and great contracts,
like the rapid transit subway. It will do
nothing of the kind. In this district about
10,000 of pur men will be affected."
STONES FOR NON-UNION" MEN.
Street Car Lines.
Police Protect Imported Labor for
ALBANY, N. Y., May 13.â Under the
escort of a platoon of mounted pollcs and
surrounded by a mob of 2000 Albanians,
200 non-union men were taken at mid
nigfht to the Quail-street car barns of the
United Traction Company, which is the
main station . of the Albany lines of the
United Traction Company. Stones were
hurled at the non-union men as they
were taken to the barns.
This is the first step taken by the com
pany to operate its lines with non-union
men. The officials of the company an
nounce that traffic will be resumed in the
morning. The non-union men, it Is said,
came from Philadelphia and were met
at the station by a platoon of mounted
officers and two patrol wagons full of
policemen.
The directors of the company remained
in session all afternoon, awaiting a reply
from the strikers to a compromise prop
osition made at a conference this morn
ing 1 . The strikers of the three cities met
and unanimously agreed not to accept
the proposition. To-night the directors
gave orders to the officers to open the
roads to-morrow.
