Dr. J. B. Schwatka of Baltimore, Md.,
THE Grand Aerie, Fraternal Order
of Eagles, will convene in Pioneer
Hall this morning at 10 o'clock.
More than a hundred delegates,
representing various branches of
the order in this country, will sit in con
vention. Everything is in readiness for
the session. The hall has been tatstefully
decorated by the local Eagles. Emblems
of the, order, potted plants and flowers
have been used profusely in beautifying
the hall.
H. R. Littlefield, grand worthy president
of the organization, will call the conven
tion to order. Committees on credentials
will be appointed and will report in the
afternoon. It is not expected that much
business will be transacted the first day.
In the evening there will be a general re
ception by San Francisco Eagles at the
hall to the grand officers, delegates and
vfliting members. An excellent literary
programme has been arranged and re
freshments will be served.
A. C. Berthier, ex-Recorder of this city,
Is chairman of the committee on reception
and entertainment. He was busily en
gaged yesterday receving delegates and
making them feel at home and presenting
them with suitable badges.
One of the leading members of the order
said "yesterday that there was j a feeling
among Eagles all over the country to
place the order on a much better footing
than it has been in the past. The rapid
growth of the organization, he said, liad^
brought about the admission of a great
He is not making a strong fight for tfcÂ©
office, but has the support of a large dele
gation from the "Western States.
Dr. . Schwatka is a cousin of thÂ© late
Lieutenant Schwatka, the famous Arctic
explorer. He Is at present Sheriff of Bal
timore and Is spoken of as a coming can
didate for Mayor of that city. The doctor
was a Congressional candidate in 1893* and
was defeated by 122 votes. He is presi
dent of the Maryland Medical College- and
was elected Sheriff two years ago by tho
Democrats of his city by 90,000 majority.
He is a past president of Baltimore No. aD,
F. O. E.
Among the distinguished members of the
organization attending the Grand Aerie
are John J. Fitzgerald. Mayor of Paw
tucket, R. L; John E. Sheehan, president
of the Illustrated Police News, and Lyme
N. Hugglns of Kammerer, Wyo. Mayor
Fitzgerald has the distinction of being
cne of the youngest city executives In the
United States. He Is 29 years of age and
says he is at the head of the best city
created by the Omnipotent power and im
proved by man. He has been offered a
number of high honors In the Fraternal
Order of Eagles, but has refused them.
He Is extremely popular with the mem
bers and came to this city on a pleasure
tour. Mr. Sheehan is past president of
Aerie No. 43 of Boston. He served two
terms as president and was Instrumental
in advancing the order in the extreme
East. He is a member of the advisory
board of the typographical union and is
past president of the Neptune Athletic
Association. He is the accredited delega la
for the State of Massachusetts.

Mr. Pierce is not an organizer; he is a dis
turber, interloper and meddler. He has tried
hard to disrupt and ruin some of the best
â¢labor organizations in existence; he has prom
ised to put the Building Trades Council out of
business; he has attacked the United Brother
hood of Railway Employes and most shame
fully abused Its officers; and he it was who
urged the machinists of the Union Iron Worka
to "cinch the Scotts" by striking some three
weeks ago and preventirg- the launching of the
Ohio If the demands were not acceded to; and
it was this man Pierce who, on the â¢ same
occasion so ardently expressed the hope that
President McKinley and Irving M. Scott would
be the only persons present at the launching
of the battleship Ohio. But such expressions
and actions. do not represent the sentiment of
the organized workingmen of San Francisco.
It Is a strange and foreign voice that repre
sents nobody and which has absolutely nothing
to lose. The organized mechanics of this city
are 'Â¦ as loyal and patriotic as the best Ameri
can citizen In any community. True trades
unionism marching behind the stars and stripes
should not be confused with the Plercean
product that wabbles behind the red flag and
keeps timp to the funeral march.
No action was taken yesterday by the
executive! committee of the San Francisco
Labor Council in the matter of a, general
boycott placed in their hands â¢ at : the
meeting \|held on Friday' night: in fact,
the conditions, legal and otherwise, that
confront I them, are such that they are
compelled to make haste slowly.
Most of the blame for this unwise labor dis
turbance when traced, down to Its original
source falls on the head of Jefferson, p. Pierce,
who througrh Irony of fate holds a commission
from Samuel Gompers, president of the Ameri
can Federation of -tabor, as "general organ
izer." Mr. -Pierce came to San Francisco in
January last, ; and as he had drawn down a
stipend from the Federation of $410 for hia
organizing: expenses In Los Angeles during the
month of December, "he just took a ; little
much-needed rest," and then proceeded to recite
his stereotyped speeches for the benefit Â¦ of the
workingmen of the city at a rate of $10 per
charter. It was. easy. Restaurant employes
and other miscellaneous trades all sent for
charters â that magical Instrument which ac
cording to Pierce can in less than twenty-four
hours radically change economic conditions
that have existed for several decades. . Higher
wages, shorter hours, stringent regulations,
immediately when the charter arrives â all
these Plereean promises for $10 only; It was
cheap',' and the roll of the embryo thing, which
was so erringly called a union, swelled Into
thousands, and the head' of the man of many
promises swelled also. His first attempt to
deliver the goods took place on the first of
May, and, of course, resulted in a failure.
The effect of this failure Is what may prove
disastrous to both the unions and the indus
tries of San Francisco.
Two bodies of organized labor unions
covering different fields of action, practi
cally control the labor mart. One is the
Building Trades Council and the other
the San Francisco Labor Council. Be
tween the j two there is no harmony of
action. Louis Chester, chairman of the
executive committee of the Building
Trades Council, makes the following
statement:
Strike matters are practically at a
standstill. Every union meeting, held lasj;
night ' declared In favor of the slogan,
"More pay and leas hours." No definite
action, however, has . been taken . by tho
Cooks' and Walters' Alliance. Secretary
Rosenberg said. last night that the San
Francisco Labor Council had so far done
nothing: to change . conditions. .â The ad
vice of Its Headers, Â¦ though sought by
workingmen, has resulted in a temporiz
ing policy.
